title="Template:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>history of Wales</b>  begins with the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Human_beings" class="mw-redirect" title="Human beings">human beings</a> in the region thousands of years ago. <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a> lived in what is now <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, or <i>Cymru</i> in <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, at least 230,000 years ago,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> arrived by about 31,000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-ncbi.nlm.nih.gov_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi.nlm.nih.gov-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> However, continuous habitation by modern humans dates from the period after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">last ice age</a> around 9000 BC, and Wales has many remains from the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Britain" title="Bronze Age Britain">Bronze Age</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">Iron Age</a> the region, like all of Britain south of the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a>, was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Celtic Britons</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Brittonic language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KochBritons_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KochBritons-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>, who began their <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">conquest of Britain</a> in AD 43, first campaigned in what is now northeast Wales in 48 against the <a href="/wiki/Deceangli" title="Deceangli">Deceangli</a>, and gained total control of the region with their defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Ordovices" title="Ordovices">Ordovices</a> in 79. The Romans <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">departed from Britain</a> in the 5th century, opening the door for the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">Anglo-Saxon invasion</a>. Thereafter Brittonic language and culture began to splinter, and several distinct groups formed. The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh people</a> were the largest of these groups, and are generally discussed independently of the other surviving <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic</a>-speaking peoples after the 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-KochBritons_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KochBritons-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A number of kingdoms formed in present-day Wales in the <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">post-Roman</a> period. While the most powerful ruler was acknowledged as <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Britons" title="King of the Britons">King of the Britons</a> (later <i><a href="/wiki/Tywysog" title="Tywysog">Tywysog Cymru</a></i>: <i>Leader</i> or <i>Prince of Wales</i>), and some rulers extended their control over other Welsh territories and into western England, none were able to unite Wales for long. Internecine struggles and external pressure from the English and later, the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman</a> conquerors of England, led to the Welsh kingdoms coming gradually under the sway of the English crown. In 1282, the death of <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ap_Gruffudd" title="Llywelyn ap Gruffudd">Llywelyn ap Gruffudd</a> led to the <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Wales_by_Edward_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Conquest of Wales by Edward I">conquest</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Wales" title="Principality of Wales">Principality of Wales</a> by King <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a>; afterwards, the <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> to the English monarch has borne the title "<a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>". The Welsh launched several revolts against English rule, the last significant one being that led by <a href="/wiki/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r" title="Owain Glynd\xc5\xb5r">Owain Glynd\xc5\xb5r</a> in the early 15th century. In the 16th century <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, himself of Welsh extraction as a great grandson of <a href="/wiki/Owen_Tudor" title="Owen Tudor">Owen Tudor</a>, passed the <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535%E2%80%931542" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535\xe2\x80\x931542">Laws in Wales Acts</a> aiming to fully incorporate Wales into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a>. Under England\'s authority, Wales became part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> in 1707 and then the United Kingdom in 1801. Yet, the Welsh retained their language and culture despite heavy English dominance. The publication of the extremely significant first complete <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Bible">Welsh translation of the Bible</a> by <a href="/wiki/William_Morgan_(Bible_translator)" title="William Morgan (Bible translator)">William Morgan</a> in 1588 greatly advanced the position of Welsh as a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Morgan_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 18th century saw the beginnings of two changes that would greatly affect Wales, the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Methodist_revival" title="Welsh Methodist revival">Welsh Methodist revival</a>, which led the country to turn increasingly <a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">nonconformist</a> in religion, and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. During the rise of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, 19th century Southeast Wales in particular experienced rapid industrialisation and a dramatic rise in population as a result of the explosion of the <a href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_Wales" title="Coal industry in Wales">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron_and_steel_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron and steel industry">iron industries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Williams174_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams174-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Wales played a full and willing role in World War One. The industries of Empire in Wales declined in the 20th century with the end of the British Empire following the Second World War, while <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">nationalist</a> sentiment and interest in self-determination rose. The <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> replaced the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> as the dominant political force in the 1940s. Wales played a considerable role during World War Two along with the rest of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland and the Allies, and its cities were bombed extensively during the Nazi Blitz. The nationalist party <a href="/wiki/Plaid_Cymru" title="Plaid Cymru">Plaid Cymru</a> gained short lived momentum in the 1960s. In a 1997 <a href="/wiki/Welsh_devolution_referendum,_1997" title="Welsh devolution referendum, 1997">referendum</a> Welsh voters approved the <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> of governmental responsibility to a <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a>, which first met in 1999.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Post-Roman_Wales_and_the_Age_of_the_Saints:_411\xe2\x80\x93700"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Post-Roman Wales and the Age of the Saints: 411\xe2\x80\x93700</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Early_Medieval_Wales:_700\xe2\x80\x931066"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Early Medieval Wales: 700\xe2\x80\x931066</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Wales_and_the_Normans:_1067\xe2\x80\x931283"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Wales and the Normans: 1067\xe2\x80\x931283</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#DNA_Research:"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">DNA Research:</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Conquest:_from_the_Statute_of_Rhuddlan_to_the_Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1283_\xe2\x80\x93_1542"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Conquest: from the Statute of Rhuddlan to the Laws in Wales Acts 1283 \xe2\x80\x93 1542</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Early_modern_period"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Early modern period</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Modern_history"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Modern history</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Population"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Population</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#1900-1914"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">1900-1914</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#1914-1945"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">1914-1945</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Since_1945"><span class="tocnumber">9.4</span> <span class="toctext">Since 1945</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Reformation"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Reformation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Nonconformity"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Nonconformity</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Further_reading_and_references"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading and references</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Religion_2"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_Wales">Prehistoric Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Prehistoric Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Wales" title="Prehistoric Wales">Prehistoric Wales</a></div>\n<p>The earliest known human remain discovered in modern-day Wales is a <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> jawbone, found at the <a href="/wiki/Bontnewydd_Palaeolithic_site" title="Bontnewydd Palaeolithic site">Bontnewydd Palaeolithic site</a> in the valley of the <a href="/wiki/River_Elwy" title="River Elwy">River Elwy</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>, whose owner lived about 230,000 years ago in the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Palaeolithic" class="mw-redirect" title="Lower Palaeolithic">Lower Palaeolithic</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-jewels_1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jewels_1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davies3_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies3-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Red_Lady_of_Paviland" title="Red Lady of Paviland">Red Lady of Paviland</a>, a human skeleton dyed in <a href="/wiki/Red_ochre" class="mw-redirect" title="Red ochre">red ochre</a>, was discovered in 1823 in one of the Paviland <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> caves of the <a href="/wiki/Gower_Peninsula" title="Gower Peninsula">Gower Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a>, South Wales. Despite the name, the skeleton is that of a young man who lived about 33,000 years ago at the end of the Upper <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> Period (old <a href="/wiki/Stone_age" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone age">stone age</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-ncbi.nlm.nih.gov_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi.nlm.nih.gov-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> He is considered to be the oldest known ceremonial burial in Western Europe. The skeleton was found along with jewellery made from <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal_shell" class="mw-redirect" title="Animal shell">seashells</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a>\'s skull.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BrynCelliDdu3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BrynCelliDdu3.jpg/250px-BrynCelliDdu3.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BrynCelliDdu3.jpg/375px-BrynCelliDdu3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BrynCelliDdu3.jpg/500px-BrynCelliDdu3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3504" data-file-height="2336" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BrynCelliDdu3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bryn Celli Ddu, a late Neolithic chambered tomb on Anglesey</div></div></div>\n<p>Following the last ice age, Wales became roughly the shape it is today by about 8000 BC and was inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>. The earliest farming communities are now believed to date from about 4000 BC, marking the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period. This period saw the construction of many <a href="/wiki/Megalithic_tomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Megalithic tomb">chambered tombs</a> particularly <a href="/wiki/Dolmen" title="Dolmen">dolmens</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Dolmen" title="Dolmen">cromlechs</a></i>. The most notable examples of megalithic tombs include <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Celli_Ddu" title="Bryn Celli Ddu">Bryn Celli Ddu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barclodiad_y_Gawres" title="Barclodiad y Gawres">Barclodiad y Gawres</a> on Anglesey,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pentre_Ifan" title="Pentre Ifan">Pentre Ifan</a> in Pembrokeshire, and <a href="/wiki/Tinkinswood" title="Tinkinswood">Tinkinswood Burial Chamber</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Glamorgan" title="Vale of Glamorgan">Vale of Glamorgan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Metal tools first appeared in Wales about 2500 BC, initially copper followed by <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>. The climate during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Early Bronze Age</a> (c. 2500\xe2\x80\x931400 BC) is thought to have been warmer than at present, as there are many remains from this period in what are now bleak uplands. The Late Bronze Age (c. 1400\xe2\x80\x93750 BC) saw the development of more advanced bronze implements. Much of the copper for the production of bronze probably came from the copper mine on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Orme" title="Great Orme">Great Orme</a>, where prehistoric mining on a very large scale dates largely from the middle Bronze Age.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon dating</a> has shown the earliest hillforts in what would become Wales, to have been constructed during this period. Historian <a href="/wiki/John_Davies_(historian)" title="John Davies (historian)">John Davies</a>, theorises that a worsening climate after around 1250 BC (lower temperatures and heavier rainfall) required more productive land to be defended.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies,_J_p._23_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies,_J_p._23-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest iron implement found in Wales is a sword from <a href="/wiki/Llyn_Fawr" title="Llyn Fawr">Llyn Fawr</a> at the head of the <a href="/wiki/River_Rhondda" title="River Rhondda">Rhondda Valley</a>, which is thought to date to about 600 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Hillforts continued to be built during the <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">British Iron Age</a>. Nearly 600 hillforts are in Wales, over 20% of those found in Britain, examples being <a href="/wiki/Pen_Dinas" title="Pen Dinas">Pen Dinas</a> near <a href="/wiki/Aberystwyth" title="Aberystwyth">Aberystwyth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tre%27r_Ceiri" title="Tre&#39;r Ceiri">Tre\'r Ceiri</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Lleyn_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Lleyn peninsula">Lleyn peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies,_J_p._23_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies,_J_p._23-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> A particularly significant find from this period was made in 1943 at <a href="/wiki/Llyn_Cerrig_Bach" title="Llyn Cerrig Bach">Llyn Cerrig Bach</a> on Anglesey, when the ground was being prepared for the construction of a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> base. The cache included weapons, shields, <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariots</a> along with their fittings and harnesses, and slave chains and tools. Many had been deliberately broken and seem to have been <a href="/wiki/Votive_offering" title="Votive offering">votive offerings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until recently, the prehistory of Wales was portrayed as a series of successive <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Wales" title="Prehistoric Wales">migrations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Morgan_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The present tendency is to stress population continuity; the <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Encyclopedia of Wales">Encyclopedia of Wales</a> suggests that Wales had received the greater part of its original stock of peoples by c.2000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Morgan_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Recent studies in <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_the_British_Isles" title="Genetic history of the British Isles">population genetics</a> have argued for genetic continuity from the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> eras.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> According to historian John Davies, the <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a> spoken throughout Britain resulted from an indigenous "cumulative Celticity", rather than from migration.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies,_J_p._23_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies,_J_p._23-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wales_in_the_Roman_era">Wales in the Roman era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Wales in the Roman era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wales_in_the_Roman_era" title="Wales in the Roman era">Wales in the Roman era</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wales.Roman.Conquest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Wales.Roman.Conquest.jpg/150px-Wales.Roman.Conquest.jpg" width="150" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Wales.Roman.Conquest.jpg/225px-Wales.Roman.Conquest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Wales.Roman.Conquest.jpg/300px-Wales.Roman.Conquest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3076" data-file-height="3080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wales.Roman.Conquest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman conquest of Wales</div></div></div>\n<p>The Roman conquest of Wales began in AD 48 and was completed in 78, with Roman rule lasting until 383. Roman rule in Wales was a military occupation, save for the southern coastal region of <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South Wales</a> east of the <a href="/wiki/Gower_Peninsula" title="Gower Peninsula">Gower Peninsula</a>, where there is a legacy of Romanisation.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The only town in Wales founded by the Romans, <a href="/wiki/Caerwent" title="Caerwent">Caerwent</a>, is located in South Wales. Both Caerwent and <a href="/wiki/Carmarthen" title="Carmarthen">Carmarthen</a>, also in southern Wales, would become Roman <i><a href="/wiki/Civitas" title="Civitas">civitates</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> During the occupation both the region that would become Wales and its people were a mostly autonomous part of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>.\n</p><p>By AD 47 Rome had invaded and conquered all of southernmost and southeastern Britain under the first <a href="/wiki/Roman_governor" title="Roman governor">Roman governor</a> of Britain. As part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">Roman conquest of Britain</a>, a series of campaigns to conquer Wales was launched by his successor in 48 and would continue intermittently under successive <a href="/wiki/Roman_governors_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman governors of Britain">governors</a> until the conquest was completed in 78. It is these campaigns of conquest that are the most widely known feature of Wales during the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman era</a> due to the spirited but unsuccessful defence of their homelands by two native tribes, the <a href="/wiki/Silures" title="Silures">Silures</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ordovices" title="Ordovices">Ordovices</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Demetae" title="Demetae">Demetae</a> of southwestern Wales seem to have quickly made their peace with the Romans, as there is no indication of war with Rome, and their homeland was not heavily planted with forts nor overlaid with roads. The Demetae would be the only Welsh tribe to emerge from Roman rule with their homeland and tribal name intact.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wales was a rich source of mineral wealth and the Romans used their <a href="/wiki/Roman_engineering" title="Roman engineering">engineering</a> <a href="/wiki/Roman_technology" title="Roman technology">technology</a> to extract large amounts of gold, copper, and lead, as well as modest amounts of some other metals such as <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and silver.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones3_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones3-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> When the mines were no longer practical or profitable, they were abandoned. Roman economic development was concentrated in southeastern Britain, with no significant industries located in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones3_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones3-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> This was largely a matter of circumstance, as Wales had none of the needed materials in suitable combination, and the forested, mountainous countryside was not amenable to development.\n</p><p>The year 383 denotes a significant point in Welsh history, remembered in literature and considered to be the foundation point of several medieval royal dynasties. In that year the Roman general <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Maximus" title="Magnus Maximus">Magnus Maximus</a> would strip all of western and northern Britain of troops and senior administrators and launch a partly successful bid for imperial power, continuing to rule Britain from <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> as emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-Frere1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frere1-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Having left with the troops and Roman administrators, and planning to continue as the ruler of Britain in the future, his practical course was to transfer local authority to local rulers. Welsh legend provides a mythic background to this process.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" width="38" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:\n<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Mabinogion/The_Dream_of_Maxen_Wledig" class="extiw" title="wikisource:The Mabinogion/The Dream of Maxen Wledig">The Dream of Maxen Wledig</a></b></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In the story of <i>Breuddwyd Macsen Wledig</i> (English: <i>The Dream of Emperor Maximus</i>), he is Emperor of Rome and marries a wondrous British woman, telling her that she may name her desires, to be received as a wedding portion. She asks that her father be given sovereignty over Britain, thus formalising the transfer of authority from Rome back to the Britons themselves. The marriage also makes possible British descendants, a point not lost on medieval kings. The earliest Welsh genealogies give Maximus the role of founding father for several royal dynasties, including those of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Powys" title="Kingdom of Powys">Powys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwent" title="Kingdom of Gwent">Gwent</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> a role he also played for the rulers of medieval <a href="/wiki/Galloway" title="Galloway">Galloway</a> in Scotland, home to the Roman-era <a href="/wiki/Novantae" title="Novantae">Novantae</a> whose territory was also made independent of Roman rule by Maximus.<sup id="cite_ref-Frere1_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frere1-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> He is given as the ancestor of a Welsh king on the <a href="/wiki/Pillar_of_Eliseg" title="Pillar of Eliseg">Pillar of Eliseg</a>, erected nearly 500 years after he left Britain, and he figures in lists of the <a href="/wiki/Fifteen_Tribes_of_Wales" title="Fifteen Tribes of Wales">Fifteen Tribes of Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rachel_Bromwich_2006_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rachel_Bromwich_2006-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CaerwentWall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/CaerwentWall.jpg/150px-CaerwentWall.jpg" width="150" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/CaerwentWall.jpg/225px-CaerwentWall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/CaerwentWall.jpg/300px-CaerwentWall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CaerwentWall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman Walls at Caerwent (<a href="/wiki/Venta_Silurum" title="Venta Silurum">Venta Silurum</a>), erected c. 350.</div></div></div>\n<p>Tradition holds that following the Roman departure, Roman customs held on into the 5th century in southern Wales, and that is true in part. Caerwent continued to be occupied, while Carmarthen was probably abandoned in the late 4th century.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In addition, southwestern Wales was the tribal territory of the Demetae, who had never become thoroughly Romanised.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones1_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> An influx of settlers from southeastern Ireland had taken place in the late 4th century,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> both in northern Wales and in the entire region of southern and southwestern Wales<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> under circumstances that are still poorly understood, and it seems far-fetched to suggest that they were ever Romanised.\n</p><p>Indeed, aside from the many Roman-related finds along the southern coast and the fully romanised area around Caerwent, Roman archaeological remains in Wales consist almost entirely of military roads and fortifications.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span id="Post-Roman_Wales_and_the_Age_of_the_Saints:_411.E2.80.93700"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Roman_Wales_and_the_Age_of_the_Saints:_411\xe2\x80\x93700">Post-Roman Wales and the Age of the Saints: 411\xe2\x80\x93700</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Post-Roman Wales and the Age of the Saints: 411\xe2\x80\x93700">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">Sub-Roman Britain</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_invasion_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon invasion of Britain">Anglo-Saxon invasion of Britain</a></div>\n<div class="floatright"><a href="/wiki/File:Britain_in_AD500_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16790.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Britain in AD500 - Project Gutenberg eText 16790.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Britain_in_AD500_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16790.jpg/200px-Britain_in_AD500_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16790.jpg" width="200" height="330" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Britain_in_AD500_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16790.jpg/300px-Britain_in_AD500_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16790.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Britain_in_AD500_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16790.jpg/400px-Britain_in_AD500_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16790.jpg 2x" data-file-width="606" data-file-height="1000" /></a></div>\n<p>When the Roman garrison of Britain was withdrawn in 410, the various British states were left self-governing. Evidence for a continuing Roman influence after the departure of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_legion" title="Roman legion">Roman legions</a> is provided by an inscribed stone from <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> dated between the late 5th century and mid 6th century commemorating a certain <a href="/wiki/Cantiorix_Inscription" title="Cantiorix Inscription">Cantiorix</a> who was described as a citizen (<i>cives</i>) of Gwynedd and a cousin of Maglos the <a href="/wiki/Magistrate" title="Magistrate">magistrate</a> (<i>magistratus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> There was considerable Irish colonisation in Dyfed in south-west Wales, where there are many stones with <a href="/wiki/Ogham" title="Ogham">Ogham</a> inscriptions.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Wales had become Christian, and the "age of the saints" (approximately 500\xe2\x80\x93700) was marked by the establishment of monastic settlements throughout the country, by religious leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Saint_David" title="Saint David">Saint David</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illtud" title="Illtud">Illtud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teilo" class="mw-redirect" title="Teilo">Teilo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cadfan.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Cadfan.JPG/250px-Cadfan.JPG" width="250" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Cadfan.JPG/375px-Cadfan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Cadfan.JPG/500px-Cadfan.JPG 2x" data-file-width="706" data-file-height="355" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cadfan.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gravestone of King <a href="/wiki/Cadfan_ap_Iago" title="Cadfan ap Iago">Cadfan ap Iago</a> of Gwynedd (died c. 625) in <a href="/wiki/Llangadwaladr" title="Llangadwaladr">Llangadwaladr</a> church</div></div></div>\n<p>One of the reasons for the Roman withdrawal was the pressure put upon the empire\'s military resources by the incursion of barbarian tribes from the east. These tribes, including the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, who later became the English, were unable to make inroads into Wales except possibly along the Severn Valley as far as <a href="/wiki/Llanidloes" title="Llanidloes">Llanidloes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> However, they gradually conquered eastern and southern Britain. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chester" title="Battle of Chester">Battle of Chester</a> in 616, the forces of Powys and other British kingdoms were defeated by the Northumbrians under <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfrith_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x86thelfrith of Northumbria">\xc3\x86thelfrith</a>, with king <a href="/wiki/Selyf_ap_Cynan" title="Selyf ap Cynan">Selyf ap Cynan</a> among the dead. It has been suggested that this battle finally severed the land connection between Wales and the kingdoms of the <a href="/wiki/Hen_Ogledd" title="Hen Ogledd">Hen Ogledd</a> ("Old North"), the Brythonic-speaking regions of what is now southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">northern England</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Rheged" title="Rheged">Rheged</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elmet" title="Elmet">Elmet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gododdin" title="Gododdin">Gododdin</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a> was also spoken.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> From the 8th century on, Wales was by far the largest of the three remnant <a href="/wiki/Brython" class="mw-redirect" title="Brython">Brythonic</a> areas in Britain, the other two being the Hen Ogledd and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>.\n</p><p>Wales was divided into a number of separate kingdoms, the largest of these being <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwynedd" title="Kingdom of Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> in northwest Wales and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Powys" title="Kingdom of Powys">Powys</a> in east Wales. Gwynedd was the most powerful of these kingdoms in the 6th century and 7th century, under rulers such as <a href="/wiki/Maelgwn_Gwynedd" title="Maelgwn Gwynedd">Maelgwn Gwynedd</a> (died 547)<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cadwallon_ap_Cadfan" title="Cadwallon ap Cadfan">Cadwallon ap Cadfan</a> (died 634/5),<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> who in alliance with <a href="/wiki/Penda_of_Mercia" title="Penda of Mercia">Penda of Mercia</a> was able to lead his armies as far as <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a> in 633,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> defeat the local ruler <a href="/wiki/Edwin_of_Northumbria" title="Edwin of Northumbria">Edwin</a> and control it for approximately one year. When Cadwallon was killed in battle by <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald of Northumbria</a>, his successor <a href="/wiki/Cadafael_Cadomedd_ap_Cynfeddw" title="Cadafael Cadomedd ap Cynfeddw">Cadafael ap Cynfeddw</a> also allied himself with Penda against Northumbria, but thereafter Gwynedd, like the other Welsh kingdoms, was mainly engaged in defensive warfare against the growing power of <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Early_Medieval_Wales:_700.E2.80.931066"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Medieval_Wales:_700\xe2\x80\x931066">Early Medieval Wales: 700\xe2\x80\x931066</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early Medieval Wales: 700\xe2\x80\x931066">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wales_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages" title="Wales in the Early Middle Ages">Wales in the Early Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd_in_the_High_Middle_Ages#11th_century" class="mw-redirect" title="Gwynedd in the High Middle Ages">Gwynedd in the High Middle Ages \xc2\xa7&#160;11th century</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Wales.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Medieval_Wales.JPG/170px-Medieval_Wales.JPG" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Medieval_Wales.JPG/255px-Medieval_Wales.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Medieval_Wales.JPG/340px-Medieval_Wales.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1203" data-file-height="1581" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Wales.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medieval kingdoms of Wales shown within the boundaries of the present day country of Wales and not inclusive of all.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Powys" title="Kingdom of Powys">Powys</a> as the easternmost of the major kingdoms of Wales came under the most pressure from the English in <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, Shropshire and <a href="/wiki/Herefordshire" title="Herefordshire">Herefordshire</a>. This kingdom originally extended east into areas now in England, and its ancient capital, <a href="/wiki/Pengwern" title="Pengwern">Pengwern</a>, has been variously identified as modern <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a> or a site north of <a href="/wiki/Baschurch" title="Baschurch">Baschurch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> These areas were lost to the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>. The construction of the earthwork known as <a href="/wiki/Offa%27s_Dyke" title="Offa&#39;s Dyke">Offa\'s Dyke</a> (usually attributed to <a href="/wiki/Offa" class="mw-redirect" title="Offa">Offa</a>, King of <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a> in the 8th century) may have marked an agreed border.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For a single man to rule the whole country during this period was rare. This is often ascribed to the inheritance system practised in Wales. All sons received an equal share of their father\'s property (including illegitimate sons), resulting in the division of territories. However, the <a href="/wiki/Cyfraith_Hywel" title="Cyfraith Hywel">Welsh laws</a> prescribe this system of division for land in general, not for kingdoms, where there is provision for an <i>edling</i> (or heir) to the kingdom to be chosen, usually by the king. Any son, legitimate or illegitimate, could be chosen as edling and there were frequently disappointed candidates prepared to challenge the chosen heir.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Laws_of_Hywel_Dda_(f.1.v)_King_Hywel_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Laws_of_Hywel_Dda_%28f.1.v%29_King_Hywel_cropped.jpg/170px-Laws_of_Hywel_Dda_%28f.1.v%29_King_Hywel_cropped.jpg" width="170" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Laws_of_Hywel_Dda_%28f.1.v%29_King_Hywel_cropped.jpg/255px-Laws_of_Hywel_Dda_%28f.1.v%29_King_Hywel_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Laws_of_Hywel_Dda_%28f.1.v%29_King_Hywel_cropped.jpg/340px-Laws_of_Hywel_Dda_%28f.1.v%29_King_Hywel_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1665" data-file-height="2929" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Laws_of_Hywel_Dda_(f.1.v)_King_Hywel_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King Hywel Dda depicted in a 13th-century manuscript</div></div></div>\n<p>The first to rule a considerable part of Wales was <a href="/wiki/Rhodri_Mawr" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodri Mawr">Rhodri Mawr</a> (Rhodri The Great), originally king of <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> during the 9th century, who was able to extend his rule to <a href="/wiki/Powys" title="Powys">Powys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ceredigion" title="Ceredigion">Ceredigion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> On his death his realms were divided between his sons. Rhodri\'s grandson, <a href="/wiki/Hywel_Dda" title="Hywel Dda">Hywel Dda</a> (Hywel the Good), formed the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Deheubarth" title="Deheubarth">Deheubarth</a> by joining smaller kingdoms in the southwest and had extended his rule to most of Wales by 942.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> He is traditionally associated with the codification of <a href="/wiki/Cyfraith_Hywel" title="Cyfraith Hywel">Welsh law</a> at a council which he called at <a href="/wiki/Whitland" title="Whitland">Whitland</a>, the laws from then on usually being called the "Laws of Hywel". Hywel followed a policy of peace with the English. On his death in 949 his sons were able to keep control of <a href="/wiki/Deheubarth" title="Deheubarth">Deheubarth</a> but lost <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> to the traditional dynasty of this kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wales was now coming under increasing attack by <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> raiders, particularly Danish raids in the period between 950 and 1000. According to the chronicle <i><a href="/wiki/Brut_y_Tywysogion" title="Brut y Tywysogion">Brut y Tywysogion</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Gofraid_mac_Arailt" title="Gofraid mac Arailt">Godfrey Haroldson</a> carried off two thousand captives from <a href="/wiki/Anglesey" title="Anglesey">Anglesey</a> in 987, and the king of Gwynedd, <a href="/wiki/Maredudd_ab_Owain" title="Maredudd ab Owain">Maredudd ab Owain</a> is reported to have redeemed many of his subjects from slavery by paying the Danes a large ransom.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gruffydd_ap_Llywelyn" title="Gruffydd ap Llywelyn">Gruffydd ap Llywelyn</a> was the only ruler to be able to unite Wales under his rule. Originally king of Gwynedd, by 1057 he was ruler of Wales and had annexed parts of England around the border. He ruled Wales with no internal battles<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> until he was defeated by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Godwinson" title="Harold Godwinson">Harold Godwinson</a> in 1063 and killed by his own men. His territories were again divided into the traditional kingdoms.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Wales_and_the_Normans:_1067.E2.80.931283"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wales_and_the_Normans:_1067\xe2\x80\x931283">Wales and the Normans: 1067\xe2\x80\x931283</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Wales and the Normans: 1067\xe2\x80\x931283">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Gwynedd in the High Middle Ages">Gwynedd in the High Middle Ages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Wales" title="Norman invasion of Wales">Norman invasion of Wales</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Wales_by_Edward_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Conquest of Wales by Edward I">Conquest of Wales by Edward I</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Caerphilly_Castle,_Wales.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Caerphilly_Castle%2C_Wales.jpg/220px-Caerphilly_Castle%2C_Wales.jpg" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Caerphilly_Castle%2C_Wales.jpg/330px-Caerphilly_Castle%2C_Wales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Caerphilly_Castle%2C_Wales.jpg/440px-Caerphilly_Castle%2C_Wales.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1468" data-file-height="890" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Caerphilly_Castle,_Wales.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Caerphilly_Castle" title="Caerphilly Castle">Caerphilly Castle</a>. The construction of this castle between 1268 and 1271 by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_de_Clare,_6th_Earl_of_Hertford" class="mw-redirect" title="Gilbert de Clare, 6th Earl of Hertford">Gilbert de Clare</a> led to a dispute between <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ap_Gruffudd" title="Llywelyn ap Gruffudd">Llywelyn ap Gruffudd</a> and the English crown, one of the issues which led to the wars of 1277 and 1282 and the end of Welsh independence</div></div></div>\n<p>At the time of the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> in 1066, the dominant ruler in Wales was <a href="/wiki/Bleddyn_ap_Cynfyn" title="Bleddyn ap Cynfyn">Bleddyn ap Cynfyn</a>, who was king of <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Powys" title="Powys">Powys</a>. The initial Norman successes were in the south, where <a href="/wiki/William_Fitzosbern,_1st_Earl_of_Hereford" class="mw-redirect" title="William Fitzosbern, 1st Earl of Hereford">William Fitz Osbern</a> overran <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwent" title="Kingdom of Gwent">Gwent</a> before 1070. By 1074 the forces of the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Shrewsbury" title="Earl of Shrewsbury">Earl of Shrewsbury</a> were ravaging <a href="/wiki/Deheubarth" title="Deheubarth">Deheubarth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The killing of Bleddyn ap Cynfyn in 1075 led to civil war and gave the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a> an opportunity to seize lands in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>. In 1081 <a href="/wiki/Gruffudd_ap_Cynan" title="Gruffudd ap Cynan">Gruffudd ap Cynan</a>, who had just won the throne of Gwynedd from <a href="/wiki/Trahaearn_ap_Caradog" title="Trahaearn ap Caradog">Trahaearn ap Caradog</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mynydd_Carn" title="Battle of Mynydd Carn">Battle of Mynydd Carn</a> was enticed to a meeting with the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Chester" title="Earl of Chester">Earl of Chester</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Shrewsbury" title="Earl of Shrewsbury">Earl of Shrewsbury</a> and promptly seized and imprisoned, leading to the seizure of much of Gwynedd by the Normans.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In the south <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> advanced into <a href="/wiki/Dyfed" title="Dyfed">Dyfed</a> founding castles and mints at <a href="/wiki/St_David%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="St David&#39;s">St David\'s</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rhys_ap_Tewdwr" title="Rhys ap Tewdwr">Rhys ap Tewdwr</a> of Deheubarth was killed in 1093 in <a href="/wiki/Brycheiniog" title="Brycheiniog">Brycheiniog</a>, and his kingdom was seized and divided between various Norman lordships.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The Norman conquest of Wales appeared virtually complete.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RhysapG.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/RhysapG.png/160px-RhysapG.png" width="160" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/RhysapG.png/240px-RhysapG.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/RhysapG.png 2x" data-file-width="312" data-file-height="461" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RhysapG.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Effigy wrongly alleged to be of Rhys ap Gruffydd in <a href="/wiki/St_David%27s_Cathedral" title="St David&#39;s Cathedral">St David\'s Cathedral</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1094, however, there was a general Welsh revolt against Norman rule, and gradually territories were won back. <a href="/wiki/Gruffudd_ap_Cynan" title="Gruffudd ap Cynan">Gruffudd ap Cynan</a> was eventually able to build a strong kingdom in <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a>. His son, <a href="/wiki/Owain_Gwynedd" title="Owain Gwynedd">Owain Gwynedd</a>, allied with <a href="/wiki/Gruffydd_ap_Rhys" title="Gruffydd ap Rhys">Gruffydd ap Rhys</a> of Deheubarth won a crushing victory over the Normans at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Crug_Mawr" title="Battle of Crug Mawr">Battle of Crug Mawr</a> in 1136 and annexed <a href="/wiki/Ceredigion" title="Ceredigion">Ceredigion</a>. Owain followed his father on the throne of Gwynedd the following year and ruled until his death in 1170.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> He was able to profit from disunity in England, where King <a href="/wiki/Stephen_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen of England">Stephen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Empress Matilda</a> were engaged in a struggle for the throne, to extend the borders of Gwynedd further east than ever before.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Powys" title="Kingdom of Powys">Powys</a> also had a strong ruler at this time in <a href="/wiki/Madog_ap_Maredudd" title="Madog ap Maredudd">Madog ap Maredudd</a>, but when his death in 1160 was quickly followed by the death of his heir, <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ap_Madog" title="Llywelyn ap Madog">Llywelyn ap Madog</a>, Powys was split into two parts and never subsequently reunited.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In the south, <a href="/wiki/Gruffydd_ap_Rhys" title="Gruffydd ap Rhys">Gruffydd ap Rhys</a> was killed in 1137, but his four sons, who all ruled Deheubarth in turn, were eventually able to win back most of their grandfather\'s kingdom from the Normans. The youngest of the four, <a href="/wiki/Rhys_ap_Gruffydd" title="Rhys ap Gruffydd">Rhys ap Gruffydd</a> (The Lord Rhys) ruled from 1155 to 1197. In 1171 Rhys met <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">King Henry II</a> and came to an agreement with him whereby Rhys had to pay a tribute but was confirmed in all his conquests and was later named <a href="/wiki/Justiciar_of_South_Wales" title="Justiciar of South Wales">Justiciar of South Wales</a>. Rhys held a festival of poetry and song at his court at <a href="/wiki/Cardigan,_Ceredigion" title="Cardigan, Ceredigion">Cardigan</a> over Christmas 1176 which is generally regarded as the first recorded <a href="/wiki/Eisteddfod" title="Eisteddfod">Eisteddfod</a>. Owain Gwynedd\'s death led to the splitting of Gwynedd between his sons, while Rhys made Deheubarth dominant in Wales for a time.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Llywelyn5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Llywelyn5.jpg/180px-Llywelyn5.jpg" width="180" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Llywelyn5.jpg/270px-Llywelyn5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Llywelyn5.jpg/360px-Llywelyn5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2336" data-file-height="3504" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Llywelyn5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Llywelyn Monument at Cilmeri</div></div></div>\n<p>Out of the power struggle in Gwynedd eventually arose one of the greatest of Welsh leaders, <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ab_Iorwerth" class="mw-redirect" title="Llywelyn ab Iorwerth">Llywelyn ab Iorwerth</a>, also known as <b>Llywelyn <i>Fawr</i></b> (the Great), who was sole ruler of Gwynedd by 1200<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and by his death in 1240 was effectively ruler of much of Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Llywelyn made his \'capital\' and headquarters at <a href="/wiki/Abergwyngregyn" title="Abergwyngregyn">Abergwyngregyn</a> on the north coast, overlooking the <a href="/wiki/Menai_Strait" title="Menai Strait">Menai Strait</a>. His son <a href="/wiki/Dafydd_ap_Llywelyn" title="Dafydd ap Llywelyn">Dafydd ap Llywelyn</a> followed him as ruler of Gwynedd, but king <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III of England</a> would not allow him to inherit his father\'s position elsewhere in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> War broke out in 1241 and then again in 1245, and the issue was still in the balance when Dafydd died suddenly at Abergwyngregyn, without leaving an heir in early 1246. Llywelyn the Great\'s other son, <a href="/wiki/Gruffudd_ap_Llywelyn_Fawr" class="mw-redirect" title="Gruffudd ap Llywelyn Fawr">Gruffudd</a> had been killed trying to escape from the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> in 1244. Gruffudd had left four sons, and a period of internal conflict between three of these ended in the rise to power of <a href="/wiki/Llywelyn_ap_Gruffudd" title="Llywelyn ap Gruffudd">Llywelyn ap Gruffudd</a> (also known as <i>Llywelyn Ein Llyw Olaf</i>; <i>Llywelyn, Our Last Leader</i>). The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Montgomery" title="Treaty of Montgomery">Treaty of Montgomery</a> in 1267 confirmed Llywelyn in control, directly or indirectly, over a large part of Wales. However, Llywelyn\'s claims in Wales conflicted with <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a>, and war followed in 1277. Llywelyn was obliged to seek terms, and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Aberconwy" title="Treaty of Aberconwy">Treaty of Aberconwy</a> greatly restricted his authority. War broke out again when Llywelyn\'s brother <a href="/wiki/Dafydd_ap_Gruffudd" class="mw-redirect" title="Dafydd ap Gruffudd">Dafydd ap Gruffudd</a> attacked <a href="/wiki/Hawarden_Castle_(medieval)" title="Hawarden Castle (medieval)">Hawarden Castle</a> on <a href="/wiki/Palm_Sunday" title="Palm Sunday">Palm Sunday</a> 1282. On 11 December 1282, Llywelyn was lured into a meeting in <a href="/wiki/Builth_Wells" title="Builth Wells">Builth Wells</a> <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castle</a> with unknown Marchers, where he was killed and his army subsequently destroyed. His brother Dafydd ap Gruffudd continued an increasingly forlorn resistance. He was captured in June 1283 and was <a href="/wiki/Hanged,_drawn_and_quartered" title="Hanged, drawn and quartered">hanged, drawn and quartered</a> at <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a>. In effect Wales became England\'s first colony until it was finally annexed through the <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535-1542" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535-1542">Laws in Wales Acts 1535-1542</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_Research:">DNA Research:</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Wales&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: DNA Research:">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Recent DNA research conducted by CymruDNA Wales has shown that a percentage of Welshmen living today are descended from ancient Kings and Princes of Wales, the quintessential DNA signature R-L371 aka S300 snp downstream from R1b-L21 (S145) is believed to have originated in North Wales around 1000 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="European_Middle_Ages_by_region" style="padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li Anatolia">Byzantine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seljuq_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Seljuq Empire">Seljuq</a>-<a href="/wiki/Seljuq_Sultanate_of_R%C3%BBm" class="mw-redirect" title="Seljuq Sultanate of R\xc3\xbbm">Rum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a>)</li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table><div></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="navbox-group" class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Frankish Empire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(medieval)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Italy (medieval)">Kingdom of Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_the_Old_Swiss_Confederacy"
